<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The sense of desolation returns, in what Hogwood calls the "remote and barbarous" key of B flat minor, for the tenor recitative "All they that see him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[122]</a></sup> The sombre sequence finally ends with the Ascension chorus "Lift up your heads", which Handel initially divides between two choral groups, the altos serving both as the bass line to a soprano choir and the treble line to the tenors and basses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[123]</a></sup> For the 1754 Foundling Hospital performance Handel added two horns, which join in when the chorus unites towards the end of the number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">[45]</a></sup> After the celebratory tone of Christ's reception into heaven, marked by the choir's D major acclamation "Let all the angels of God worship him", the "<a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Whitsun</a>" section proceeds through a series of contrasting moods—serene and pastoral in "How beautiful are the feet", theatrically operatic in "Why do the nations so furiously rage"—towards the Part II culmination of "Hallelujah". This, as Young points out, is not the climactic chorus of the work, although one cannot escape its "contagious enthusiasm".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[124]</a></sup> It builds from a deceptively light orchestral opening,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">[45]</a></sup> through a short, unison <a href="/wiki/Cantus_firmus" title="Cantus firmus">cantus firmus</a> passage on the words "For the Lord God omnipotent reigneth", to the reappearance of the long-silent trumpets at "And He shall reign for ever and ever". Commentators have noted that the musical line for this third subject is based on <i><a href="/wiki/Wachet_auf,_ruft_uns_die_Stimme" title="Wachet auf, ruft uns die Stimme">Wachet auf</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Nicolai" title="Philipp Nicolai">Philipp Nicolai</a>'s popular <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> <a href="/wiki/Chorale" title="Chorale">chorale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-6"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Part_III">Part III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Part III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Messiah_Part_III" title="Messiah Part III">Messiah Part III</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="444" data-file-width="545" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg/220px-Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg/330px-Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg/440px-Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First page of the concluding chorus "Worthy is the Lamb": From Handel's manuscript</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The opening soprano solo in E major, "I know that my Redeemer liveth" is one of the few numbers in the oratorio that has remained unrevised from its original form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H26_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">[126]</a></sup> Its simple unison violin accompaniment and its consoling rhythms apparently brought tears to Burney's eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L104_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-L104-136">[127]</a></sup> It is followed by a quiet chorus that leads to the bass's declamation in D major: "Behold, I tell you a mystery", then the long aria "The trumpet shall sound", marked <i><span lang="it">pomposo ma non allegro</span></i> ("dignified but not fast").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H26_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">[126]</a></sup> Handel originally wrote this in <a href="/wiki/Da_capo" title="Da capo">da capo</a> form, but shortened it to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dal_segno" title="Dal segno">dal segno</a>, probably before the first performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[128]</a></sup> The extended, characteristic trumpet tune that precedes and accompanies the voice is the only significant instrumental solo in the entire oratorio. Handel's awkward, repeated stressing of the fourth syllable of "incorruptible" may have been the source of the 18th-century poet <a href="/wiki/William_Shenstone" title="William Shenstone">William Shenstone</a>'s comment that he "could observe some parts in <i><a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a></i> wherein Handel's judgements failed him; where the music was not equal, or was even <i>opposite</i>, to what the words required".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H26_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[129]</a></sup> After a brief solo recitative, the alto is joined by the tenor for the only duet in Handel's final version of the music, "O death, where is thy sting?" The melody is adapted from Handel's 1722 cantata <i><span lang="it">Se tu non lasci amore</span></i>, and is in Luckett's view the most successful of the Italian borrowings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L104_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-L104-136">[127]</a></sup> The duet runs straight into the chorus "But thanks be to God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H26_135-3"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The reflective soprano solo "If God be for us" (originally written for alto) quotes <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Luther</a>'s chorale <i><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Aus_tiefer_Not_schrei_ich_zu_dir" title="Aus tiefer Not schrei ich zu dir">Aus tiefer Not</a></span></i>. It ushers in the D major choral finale: "Worthy is the Lamb", leading to the apocalyptic "Amen" in which, says Hogwood, "the entry of the trumpets marks the final storming of heaven".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H26_135-4"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">[126]</a></sup> Handel's first biographer, <a href="/wiki/John_Mainwaring" title="John Mainwaring">John Mainwaring</a>, wrote in 1760 that this conclusion revealed the composer "rising still higher" than in "that vast effort of genius, the Hallelujah chorus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L104_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-L104-136">[127]</a></sup> Young writes that the "Amen" should, in the manner of <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a>, "be delivered as though through the aisles and ambulatories of some great church".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recordings">Recordings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Recordings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many early recordings of individual choruses and arias from <i>Messiah</i> reflect the performance styles then fashionable—large forces, slow tempi and liberal reorchestration. Typical examples are choruses conducted by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wood" title="Henry Wood">Sir Henry Wood</a>, recorded in 1926 for <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Graphophone_Company" title="Columbia Graphophone Company">Columbia</a> with the 3,500-strong choir and orchestra of the Crystal Palace Handel Festival, and a contemporary rival disc from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HMV_Records" title="HMV Records">HMV</a> featuring the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Choral_Society" title="Royal Choral Society">Royal Choral Society</a> under Malcolm Sargent, recorded at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Hall" title="Royal Albert Hall">Royal Albert Hall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first near-complete recording of the whole work (with the cuts then customary)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[n 10]</a></sup> was conducted by Sir Thomas Beecham in 1928. It represented an effort by Beecham to "provide an interpretation which, in his opinion, was nearer the composer's intentions", with smaller forces and faster tempi than had become traditional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klein_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-klein-102">[94]</a></sup> His contralto soloist, <a href="/wiki/Muriel_Brunskill" title="Muriel Brunskill">Muriel Brunskill</a>, later commented, "His tempi, which are now taken for granted, were revolutionary; he entirely revitalised it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blyth_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">[88]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Sargent retained the large scale tradition in his four HMV recordings, the first in 1946 and three more in the 1950s and 1960s, all with the <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_Choral_Society" title="Huddersfield Choral Society">Huddersfield Choral Society</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Liverpool_Philharmonic" title="Royal Liverpool Philharmonic">Liverpool Philharmonic Orchestra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blyth_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">[88]</a></sup> Beecham's second recording of the work, in 1947, "led the way towards more truly Handelian rhythms and speeds", according to the critic <a href="/wiki/Alan_Blyth" title="Alan Blyth">Alan Blyth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blyth_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">[88]</a></sup> In a 1991 study of all 76 complete <i>Messiahs</i> recorded by that date, the writer Teri Noel Towe called this version of Beecham's "one of a handful of truly stellar performances".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blyth_95-4"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1954 the first recording based on Handel's original scoring was conducted by <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Scherchen" title="Hermann Scherchen">Hermann Scherchen</a> for <a href="/wiki/Nixa_Records" title="Nixa Records">Nixa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[n 11]</a></sup> quickly followed by a version, judged scholarly at the time, under Sir Adrian Boult for <a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rg_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-rg-141">[132]</a></sup> By the standards of 21st-century performance, however, Scherchen's and Boult's tempi were still slow, and there was no attempt at vocal ornamentation by the soloists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rg_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-rg-141">[132]</a></sup> In 1966 and 1967 two new recordings were regarded as great advances in scholarship and performance practice, conducted respectively by <a href="/wiki/Colin_Davis" title="Colin Davis">Colin Davis</a> for <a href="/wiki/Philips_Records" title="Philips Records">Philips</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mackerras" title="Charles Mackerras">Charles Mackerras</a> for HMV. They inaugurated a new tradition of brisk, small scale performances, with vocal embellishments by the solo singers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[n 12]</a></sup> Among recordings of older-style performances are Beecham's 1959 recording with the Royal Philharmonic Orchestra, with orchestration commissioned from Sir Eugene Goossens and completed by the English composer <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Salzedo" title="Leonard Salzedo">Leonard Salzedo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blyth_95-5"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Richter_(conductor)" title="Karl Richter (conductor)">Karl Richter</a>'s 1973 version for <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Grammophon" title="Deutsche Grammophon">DG</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[135]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/David_Willcocks" title="David Willcocks">David Willcocks</a>'s 1995 performance based on Prout's 1902 edition of the score, with a 325-voice choir and 90-piece orchestra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of the 1970s the quest for authenticity had extended to the use of period instruments and historically correct styles of playing them. The first of such versions were conducted by the early music specialists <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hogwood" title="Christopher Hogwood">Christopher Hogwood</a> (1979) and <a href="/wiki/John_Eliot_Gardiner" title="John Eliot Gardiner">John Eliot Gardiner</a> (1982).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[137]</a></sup> The use of period instruments quickly became the norm on record, although some conductors, among them <a href="/wiki/Georg_Solti" title="Georg Solti">Sir Georg Solti</a> (1985), continued to favour modern instruments. <i><a href="/wiki/Gramophone_(magazine)" title="Gramophone (magazine)">Gramophone</a></i> magazine and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Penguin_Guide_to_Recorded_Classical_Music" title="The Penguin Guide to Recorded Classical Music">The Penguin Guide to Recorded Classical Music</a></i> highlighted two versions, conducted respectively by <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Pinnock" title="Trevor Pinnock">Trevor Pinnock</a> (1988) and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hickox" title="Richard Hickox">Richard Hickox</a> (1992). The latter employs a chorus of 24 singers and an orchestra of 31 players; Handel is known to have used a chorus of 19 and an orchestra of 37.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[138]</a></sup> Performances on an even smaller scale have followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[n 13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several reconstructions of early performances have been recorded: the 1742 Dublin version by Scherchen in 1954, and again in 1959, and by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Malgoire" title="Jean-Claude Malgoire">Jean-Claude Malgoire</a> in 1980<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[141]</a></sup> There are several recordings of the 1754 Foundling Hospital version, including those under Hogwood (1979), <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Parrott" title="Andrew Parrott">Andrew Parrott</a> (1989), and Paul McCreesh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Presto_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Presto-156">[143]</a></sup> In 1973 David Willcocks conducted a set for HMV in which all the soprano arias were sung in unison by the boys of the <a href="/wiki/Choir_of_King%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="Choir of King's College, Cambridge">Choir of King's College, Cambridge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[144]</a></sup> and in 1974, for DG, Mackerras conducted a set of Mozart's reorchestrated version, sung in German.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blyth_95-6"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Editions">Editions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Editions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first published score of 1767, together with Handel's documented adaptations and recompositions of various movements, has been the basis for many performing versions since the composer's lifetime. Modern performances which seek authenticity tend to be based on one of three 20th-century performing editions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bix_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">[107]</a></sup> These all use different methods of numbering movements:</p>
<ul>
<li>The Novello Edition, edited by Watkins Shaw, first published as a vocal score in 1959, revised and issued 1965. This uses the numbering first used in the Prout edition of 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bix_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Bärenreiter Edition, edited by John Tobin, published in 1965, which forms the basis of the <i>Messiah</i> numbering in <a href="/wiki/Bernd_Baselt" title="Bernd Baselt">Bernd Baselt</a>'s catalogue (HWV) of Handel's works, published in 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bix_116-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Peters Edition, edited by Donald Burrows, vocal score published 1972, which uses an adaptation of the numbering devised by Kurt Soldan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bix_116-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Van Camp Edition, edited by Leonard Van Camp, published by Roger Dean Publishing, 1993 rev. 1995 (now Lorenz pub.).</li>
<li>The Oxford University Press edition by Clifford Bartlett, 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Carus-Verlag Edition, edited by Ton Koopman and Jan H. Siemons, published in 2009 (using the HWV numbering).</li>
</ul>
<p>The edition edited by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Chrysander" title="Friedrich Chrysander">Friedrich Chrysander</a> and <a href="/wiki/Max_Seiffert" title="Max Seiffert">Max Seiffert</a> for the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4ndel-Gesellschaft" title="Händel-Gesellschaft">Deutsche Händel-Gesellschaft</a> (Berlin, 1902) is not a general performing edition, but has been used as a basis of scholarship and research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bix_116-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to Mozart's well-known reorchestration, arrangements for larger orchestral forces exist by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Aynsley_Goossens" title="Eugene Aynsley Goossens">Goossens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Davis_(conductor)" title="Andrew Davis (conductor)">Andrew Davis</a>; both have been recorded at least once, on the <a href="/wiki/RCA_Records" title="RCA Records">RCA</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[146]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Chandos_Records" title="Chandos Records">Chandos</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[147]</a></sup> labels respectively.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_and_references">Notes and references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notes and references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Notes</b></p>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 24em; -webkit-column-width: 24em; column-width: 24em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since its earliest performances the work has often been referred to, incorrectly, as "The <i>Messiah</i>". The article is absent from the proper title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The description "Sinfony" is taken from Handel's autograph score.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is possible that Delaney was alluding to the fact that Cibber was, at that time, involved in a scandalous divorce suit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-48">[46]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Anthony_Hicks" title="Anthony Hicks">Anthony Hicks</a> gives a slightly different instrumentation: 14 violins and 6 violas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Swieten provided Mozart with a London publication of Handel's original orchestration (published by Randal &amp; Abell), as well as a German translation of the English libretto, compiled and created by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Gottlieb_Klopstock" title="Friedrich Gottlieb Klopstock">Friedrich Gottlieb Klopstock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Daniel_Ebeling" title="Christoph Daniel Ebeling">Christoph Daniel Ebeling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A repeat performance was given in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esterh%C3%A1za" title="Esterháza">Esterháza</a> court on 7 April 1789,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> and between the year of Mozart's death (1791) and 1800, there were four known performances of Mozart's re-orchestrated <i>Messiah</i> in Vienna: 5 April 1795, 23 March 1799, 23 December 1799 and 24 December 1799.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hiller was long thought to have revised Mozart's scoring substantially before the score was printed. Ebenezer Prout pointed out that the edition was published as "F. G.  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] Händels Oratorium Der Messias, nach W. A. Mozarts Bearbeitung" – "nach" meaning <i>after</i> rather than <i>in</i> Mozart's arrangement. Prout noted that a Mozart edition of another Handel work, <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander%27s_Feast_(Handel)" title="Alexander's Feast (Handel)">Alexander's Feast</a></i> published in accordance with Mozart's manuscript, was printed as "mit neuer Bearbeitung von W. A. Mozart" ("with new arrangement by W. A. Mozart)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prout1_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-prout1-81">[75]</a></sup> When Mozart's original manuscript subsequently came to light it was found that Hiller's changes were not extensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[76]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Many of the editions before 1902, including Mozart's, derived from the earliest printed edition of the score, known as the Walsh Edition, published in 1767.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prout1_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-prout1-81">[75]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1966 an edition by John Tobin was published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dean_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-dean-109">[101]</a></sup> More recent editions have included those edited by Donald Burrows (Edition Peters, 1987) and Clifford Bartlett (Oxford University Press, 1999).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The numbers customarily omitted were: from Part II, "Unto which of the angels"; "Let all the angels of God worship Him"; and "Thou art gone up on high"; and from Part III, "Then shall be brought to pass"; "O death, where is thy sting?", "But thanks be to God"; and "If God be for us".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rg_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-rg-141">[132]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This recording was monophonic and issued on commercial CD by PRT in 1986; Scherchen re-recorded Messiah in stereo in 1959 using Vienna forces; this was issued on LP by Westminster and on commercial CD by Deutsche Grammophon in 2001. Both recordings have appeared on other labels in both LP and CD formats. A copyright-free transfer of the 1954 version (digitized from original vinyl discs by Nixa Records) is available on YouTube: <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=zhb3s4E8-CA" rel="nofollow">part 1</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gfQnICqY3j4" rel="nofollow">part 2</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=M48k_QBXKeU" rel="nofollow">part 3</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Davis set uses a chorus of 40 singers and an orchestra of 39 players;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[133]</a></sup> the Mackerras set uses similarly sized forces, but with fewer strings and more wind players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[134]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A 1997 recording under Harry Christophers employed a chorus of 19 and an orchestra of 20.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[139]</a></sup> In 1993, the Scholars Baroque Ensemble released a version with 14 singers including soloists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[140]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
